Let's consider a more systematic approach. The problem is from a contest? Possibly the answer is c = 4. I recall a known problem: "A function f: ℕ → ℕ is called bonza if f(a) divides b^a - f(b)^{f(a)} for all a,b. Find the smallest real c such that f(n) ≤ cn for all bonza f and all n." The solution likely uses the trick that for any a, we can choose b such that f(b) = 1 (maybe using the fact that f(n) = 1 for infinitely many n, or something). But we don't know that. However we can try to prove that f(n) ≤ 4n by considering the following:

Take a = n, b = 2. Then:

f(n) | 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}.

Now, note that f(2) is either 1,2,4. If f(2) = 1, then f(n) | 2^n - 1. That's not helpful. If f(2) = 2, then f(n) | 2^n - 2^{f(n)}. If f(2) = 4, then f(n) | 2^n - 4^{f(n)}.

Now, consider also a = n, b = 3. Then:

f(n) | 3^n - f(3)^{f(n)}.

Similarly, a = n, b = 4: f(n) | 4^n - f(4)^{f(n)}. But we might not know f(3), f(4). However we can try to bound f(n) by something like the maximum of (2^n, 3^n) but that's huge.

Maybe we can use the fact that f(n) divides n^n, so if f(n) is large, then n has many prime factors. Then we can consider a prime p such that p does not divide n. Then from a = n, b = p, we get f(n) | p^n - f(p)^{f(n)}. If we can prove that f(p) = 1 for all primes p, then f(n) | p^n - 1. Now, how to prove that? Let's think about possible strategies.

Observation: The condition is reminiscent of a functional equation that forces f to be something like f(n) = n^{something} but not linear. However we have the bound f(2) ≤ 4, which is 2*2. That's linear. But we need to prove that for all n, f(n) ≤ 4n. Perhaps we can prove that f(n) ≤ n * (something like the maximum of something). But let's try to derive an inequality.

Suppose we have a bonza function f. Consider any a. Let d = f(a). Then from a = b = a, we have d | a^a. So d ≤ a^a. That's not helpful. But also from b = 2, we have d | 2^a - f(2)^d. Since f(2) is small. ## Solution
Let $\mathbb{N}=\{1,2,3,\dots\}$.  A function $f:\mathbb{N}\to\mathbb{N}$ is called **bonza** if for all $a,b\in\mathbb{N}$,
\[
f(a)\mid\bigl(b^{\,a}-f(b)^{\,f(a)}\bigr).
\tag{1}
\]

We shall determine the smallest real constant $c$ such that $f(n)\le c\,n$ for every bonza function $f$ and every $n\in\mathbb{N}$.

---
### 1.  Basic properties and a lower bound

First we derive some necessary conditions.

*Put $a=b=1$ in (1).*  $f(1)\mid 1-f(1)^{f(1)}$, hence $f(1)\mid 1$.  Since $f(1)\in\mathbb{N}$, we must have $f(1)=1$.

*Put $a=b=2$.*  $f(2)\mid 4-f(2)^{f(2)}$, so $f(2)\mid 4$.  Consequently
\[
f(2)\in\{1,2,4\}.
\tag{2}
\]

Now construct a concrete bonza function to obtain a lower bound for the optimal constant $c$.  
Define
\[
f(1)=1,\qquad 
f(2)=4,\qquad 
f(3)=1,\qquad 
f(4)=16,\qquad 
f(5)=1,\qquad 
f(6)=2,\qquad 
f(7)=1,\qquad 
f(8)=2,\qquad 
f(9)=1,\qquad \dots
\]
More precisely, for $n\ge 1$:
\[
f(n)=
\begin{cases}
4 & \text{if } n=2,\\
16 & \text{if } n=4,\\
2 & \text{if } n\ge 6 \text{ and } n \text{ is even},\\
1 & \text{if } n \text{ is odd}.
\end{cases}
\]
(For $n=2$ we have $f(2)=4$, for $n=4$ we have $f(4)=16$, all other even numbers $\ge6$ get $f(n)=2$, and all odd numbers get $f(n)=1$.)

We verify that this function satisfies (1).  The only nontrivial checks involve pairs where $f(a)$ is not $1$; a direct computation shows:

- $a=2$, $f(2)=4$:  $4\mid b^{2}-f(b)^{4}$ for all $b$.  
  If $b$ is odd then $f(b)=1$ and $b^{2}\equiv1\pmod4$, so $b^{2}-1\equiv0\pmod4$;  
  if $b$ is even then $f(b)=2$ and $b^{2}\equiv0\pmod4$, $2^{4}=16\equiv0\pmod4$, hence $b^{2}-16\equiv0\pmod4$.  
  Thus $4\mid b^{2}-f(b)^{4}$.

- $a=4$, $f(4)=16$:  $16\mid b^{4}-f(b)^{16}$.  
  If $b$ is odd then $f(b)=1$ and $b^{4}\equiv1\pmod{16}$ (odd fourth powers are $1$ mod 16), so $b^{4}-1\equiv0\pmod{16}$;  
  if $b$ is even then $f(b)=2$ and $b^{4}\equiv0\pmod{16}$, $2^{16}\equiv0\pmod{16}$, hence $b^{4}-2^{16}\equiv0\pmod{16}$.

- $a\ge6$ even, $f(a)=2$:  $2\mid b^{a}-f(b)^{2}$ for all $b$.  
  If $b$ is odd then $f(b)=1$ and $b^{a}$ is odd, $1^{2}=1$, difference even;  
  if $b$ is even then $f(b)=2$ and $b^{a}$ is even, $2^{2}=4$ even, difference even.

All other pairs are trivial because $f(a)=1$.  Hence this function is bonza.  For it we have $f(4)=16=4\cdot4$, so the constant $c$ cannot be smaller than $4$:
\[
c\ge4.
\tag{3}
\]

---
### 2.  An upper bound: $f(n)\le 4n$ for every bonza $f$

We now prove that for any bonza function $f$ and any $n\in\mathbb{N}$,
\[
f(n)\le 4n.
\]

The proof is by induction on $n$ and relies on the following two lemmas.

**Lemma 1.**  For every $a\in\mathbb{N}$,
\[
f(a)\mid a^{\,a}.
\]

*Proof.*  Set $b=a$ in (1).  Then $f(a)\mid a^{\,a}-f(a)^{f(a)}$.  Since $f(a)\mid f(a)^{f(a)}$, we obtain $f(a)\mid a^{\,a}$. ∎

**Lemma 2.**  For every $n\ge1$,
\[
f(n)\le 2^{\,v_{2}(n)}\cdot 3^{\,v_{3}(n)}.
\]

(Here $v_{p}(n)$ denotes the exponent of the prime $p$ in $n$.)  

*Proof.*  We consider the congruence (1) with $b=n$.  Because $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$ we can write $n^{\,n}=f(n)\,m$ with $m\in\mathbb{N}$.  The condition (1) with $a=n$ and $b=n$ already gave Lemma 1.  The crucial observation is that $f(n)$ must divide $n^{\,n}$, so all prime factors of $f(n)$ are among the primes dividing $n$.  Moreover, from (1) with $a=n$ and $b=2$ we have
\[
f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-f(2)^{\,f(n)}.
\tag{4}
\]

Now $f(2)\in\{1,2,4\}$ by (2).  If $f(2)=1$ then (4) gives $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-1$.  If $f(2)=2$ then $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-2^{\,f(n)}$, which forces $v_{2}(f(n))\le n$ and also $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-2^{\,f(n)}$; a more refined analysis shows that the only possible values of $f(n)$ are powers of $2$ with exponent at most $n$ (this will be used later).  If $f(2)=4$ then $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-4^{\,f(n)}$.

We now prove the bound $f(n)\le 2^{\,v_{2}(n)}\cdot 3^{\,v_{3}(n)}$ by considering the prime factorisation of $n$.  Let $p$ be any prime.  Because $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$, we have $p$ divides $f(n)$ only if $p$ divides $n$, and then $v_{p}(f(n))\le n\,v_{p}(n)$.  Hence the size of $f(n)$ is controlled by the sizes of the prime powers dividing $n$.  The constant $4$ appears naturally because $f(2)\le 4$ and $f(3)\le 9$ (we will bound $f(3)$ later).  However, a more elegant argument is to note that $f(n)$ must also divide $3^{\,n}-f(3)^{\,f(n)}$ and, together with the bound on $f(2)$ and $f(3)$, one can derive $f(n)\le 4n$ directly.  Let us present a self‑contained induction.

---
**Induction hypothesis.**  For all $m<n$, $f(m)\le 4m$.

**Induction step.**  We want to prove $f(n)\le 4n$.

We know $f(2)\le 4$ and $f(3)\le 4\cdot3=12$ (this will be proved below).  The main difficulty is to bound $f(n)$ when $n$ is large.  A key idea is to use the fact that $f(a)$ divides $a^{\,a}$, so in particular $f(n)\le n^{\,n}$.  But we need a linear bound.  The induction hypothesis does not directly give a bound for $f(n)$ because it involves numbers smaller than $n$; however we can combine the divisibility conditions with $b=2$ and $b=3$ to obtain inequalities that force $f(n)\le 4n$.

Consider the congruence (1) with $a=n$ and $b=2$:
\[
f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-f(2)^{\,f(n)}.
\tag{4}
\]
Similarly, with $b=3$:
\[
f(n)\mid 3^{\,n}-f(3)^{\,f(n)}.
\tag{5}
\]

Now, we already know $f(2)\le 4$ and $f(3)\le 4\cdot3=12$ (the latter follows from Lemma 1 applied to $a=3$ and a direct check of possible values; we shall prove $f(3)\le 3$ when $f(2)=2$, but in any case $f(3)\le 9$, so $f(3)\le 12$).  For the purpose of the induction we can assume $f(3)\le 12$ (which is true because $f(3)\mid 27$, and the only possible values are $1,3,9,27$, but $27$ cannot occur because it would violate (1) with $a=3,b=2$ – we will check that later).  So $f(3)\le 9\le 12$.

Now, suppose $f(n)>4n$.  Then $f(n)\ge 4n+1$.  Because $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$, we have $n^{\,n}\ge f(n)\ge 4n+1$, which is true for $n\ge 2$.  The interesting part is the consequence of (4).  If $f(2)=4$, then $2^{\,n}\equiv 4^{\,f(n)}\pmod{f(n)}$, i.e. $2^{\,n}\equiv 2^{\,2f(n)}\pmod{f(n)}$.  This implies $2^{\,2f(n)-n}\equiv 1\pmod{f(n)}$ (provided $2f(n)\ge n$; otherwise we can rearrange).  Hence the order of $2$ modulo $f(n)$ divides $|2f(n)-n|$.  But $f(n)$ is large, so this forces $f(n)$ to be a divisor of $2^{\,|2f(n)-n|}-1$, which is a number that is astronomically larger than $f(n)$.  That does not give a contradiction.  However, we can obtain a contradiction by looking at the smallest prime factor of $n$ and using a lower bound on $f(n)$ derived from (4) and (5) together with the fact that $f(n)$ divides $n^{\,n}$.

A more systematic approach:  We will prove that $f(n)\le 4n$ by induction on $n$ and using the following auxiliary claim.

**Claim.**  For any $n\ge 1$, $f(n)\le 4n$.

*Proof.*  We already know $f(1)=1\le4$, $f(2)\le4$, $f(3)\le3$ (we need to check $f(3)$).  So assume $n\ge4$.  From Lemma 1, $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$.  Write $n^{\,n}=f(n)\,k$ with $k\in\mathbb{N}$.  If $f(n)\le 4n$, we are done.  So suppose $f(n)>4n$.  Then $k< n^{\,n-1}/4$.  This is not contradictory.  But we can also use the condition with $b=n-1$?  That would involve $f(n-1)$ which by induction is $\le4(n-1)$.  Then $f(n)\mid (n-1)^{\,n}-f(n-1)^{\,f(n)}$.  Since $f(n-1)\le 4(n-1)$, we have $f(n-1)^{\,f(n)}$ is huge if $f(n)$ is large.  This could force $f(n)$ to be a divisor of a number that is too small?  Not.

Let us instead prove the stronger statement:  For every $n$, $f(n)\le n\cdot \max\{f(2), f(3)\}$?  But $f(2)\le4$, $f(3)\le 9$, so that would give $f(n)\le 9n$, not $4n$.  So not.

I need to present a rigorous proof.  Perhaps the intended solution is to prove $f(n)\le 2n$ for all $n$ except maybe $n=4$, but we have a counterexample with $f(4)=16$.  So that's false.  Therefore, the bound $4n$ must be proven by a clever argument that uses the fact that $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$ and also $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-f(2)^{\,f(n)}$, and then by considering the $2$-adic and $3$-adic valuations one can show that $f(n)\le 4n$ holds.  Let's attempt a direct inequality.

Suppose $f(n)=d$.  From $d\mid n^{\,n}$, all primes dividing $d$ are $\le n$.  Let $p$ be a prime dividing $d$.  Then $p\le n$.  Consider the congruence (1) with $b=p$.  Then $d\mid p^{\,n}-f(p)^{\,d}$.  Since $f(p)\mid p^{\,p}$ (by Lemma 1), $f(p)$ is a power of $p$.  Thus $p^{\,n}-f(p)^{\,d}\equiv 0\pmod{d}$.  In particular, $p^{\,n}\equiv f(p)^{\,d}\pmod{p^{\,v_{p}(d)}}$ (since $p$ is a prime factor of $d$).  This implies that $v_{p}(d)\le n$ (if $f(p)$ is a multiple of $p$).  But $v_{p}(d)$ could be as large as $n\,v_{p}(n)$.

Nevertheless, we can try to bound the size of $d$ by $4n$ using the following idea:  For each prime $p$, $d$ cannot have a prime factor $p$ with exponent $e$ if $p$ does not divide $n$?  That's already covered.  The key is to show that if $d>4n$, then there exists a prime $p$ dividing $n$ such that the exponent of $p$ in $d$ is too large, which would contradict the congruence with $b=2$ or $b=3$ because $2^{\,n}-4^{\,d}$ would then be too small relative to $d$?  But it's huge.

Wait, I recall a known solution to a similar problem:  The constant $c$ is $4$, and the proof uses the fact that $f(2)\le4$ and $f(3)\le 3$ (since $f(3)\mid 27$ and also $f(3)\mid 8-2^{f(3)}$, which forces $f(3)=1$ or $3$, and then by induction one shows $f(n)\le 4n$).  But we saw $f(3)=9$ is possible with $f(2)=2$.  However, we must check whether $f(3)=9$ can be part of a bonza function.  The condition $a=3,b=2$ gives $9\mid 8-2^{9}=8-512=-504$, which is not true (504/9=56, but 8-512 = -504, 504 is divisible by 9, so -504 is divisible by 9? 504/9=56, yes, 9 divides 504, so -504 is divisible by 9.  So $9\mid 8-2^{9}$? 8-512 = -504, 504/9=56, so 9 divides -504.  So $9$ divides $8-2^{9}$.  So $f(3)=9$ with $f(2)=2$ is allowed?  Let's check: $a=3,b=2$, we need $f(3)=9$ divides $2^{3}-f(2)^{f(3)} = 8-2^{9}=8-512=-504$. 504 is divisible by 9, so -504 is divisible by 9, yes.  So that's consistent.  But we also have $a=2,b=3$: $f(2)=2$ divides $3^{2}-f(3)^{2}=9-81=-72$, 2 divides -72, ok.  So $f(2)=2$, $f(3)=9$ seems consistent for those pairs.  So $f(3)$ can be $9$.  Therefore, the bound $f(3)\le 3$ is false.  So we need to handle that.

Maybe we can prove that $f(3)\le 9$ always, and then combine with $f(2)\le4$ to get $f(4)\le 16$, etc.  But for $n=6$, we need to prove $f(6)\le 24$.  The candidate with $f(6)=36$ seems to fail because of $a=7,b=6$.  So maybe the induction can be carried out with a stronger hypothesis:  For all $m<n$, $f(m)\le 4m$, and also $f(m)\le 2^{v_{2}(m)}\cdot 3^{v_{3}(m)}$.  But that's not induction.

Let's step back and think about a more clever bound.  The condition (1) with $a=n$ and $b=n$ gave $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$.  So $f(n)$ is a divisor of $n^{\,n}$.  The function $f(n)$ can be expressed as $f(n)= \prod_{p\mid n} p^{\,e_{p}(n)}$ with $e_{p}(n)\le n\,v_{p}(n)$.  Now consider the condition with $b=2$.  Then $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-f(2)^{f(n)}$.  If $f(2)=4$, then $2^{\,n}\equiv 4^{f(n)}\pmod{f(n)}$, i.e. $2^{\,n}\equiv 2^{\,2f(n)}\pmod{f(n)}$.  This implies $2^{\,2f(n)-n}\equiv 1\pmod{f(n)}$ (provided $2f(n)\ge n$).  So the multiplicative order of $2$ modulo $f(n)$ divides $2f(n)-n$.  Now, note that $f(n)$ is a divisor of $n^{\,n}$, so $f(n)$ is a product of prime powers that are ≤ $n$.  For any odd prime $p$ dividing $f(n)$, we have $p\le n$.  Also, $2$ is a primitive root modulo $p$?  Not necessarily.  But we can use the following:  If $p$ is an odd prime and $p\mid f(n)$, then $p\mid n$.  Then $2^{p-1}\equiv 1\pmod p$.  The order of $2$ modulo $p$ divides $p-1$.  Also, from the congruence, $2^{2f(n)-n}\equiv 1\pmod{p}$.  Thus the order of $2$ modulo $p$ divides $\gcd(p-1, 2f(n)-n)$.  This is a bound.  Not contradictory.

I'm going to try to prove $f(n)\le 4n$ by contradiction assuming $f(n)>4n$ and then using the fact that $f(n)\mid n^{\,n}$ and also $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-f(2)^{f(n)}$, we can derive that $f(n)$ must be a divisor of $2^{\,n}-4^{\,f(n)}$ and $n^{\,n}$, which then forces $f(n)$ to be small.  Let's examine the possibility $f(2)=4$.  Then $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}-4^{\,f(n)}$.  Factor $2^{\,n}-4^{\,f(n)} = 2^{\,n}-2^{\,2f(n)}$.  If $f(n)>n$, then $2f(n) > n$, so we can write $2^{\,n}-2^{\,2f(n)} = -2^{\,n}(2^{\,2f(n)-n}-1)$.  Thus $f(n)\mid 2^{\,n}(2^{\,2f(n)-n}-1)$.  Since $f(n)$ divides $n^{\,n}$, and $n^{\,n}$ is odd or even?  But $f(n)$ may contain powers of $2$.  The factor $2^{\,n}$ gives $v_{2}(f(n))\le n$.  That's okay.
